		<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading mw-first-heading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Dune</span></h1>
		<div id="bodyContent" class="vector-body">
			<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
			<div id="contentSub"></div>
			<div id="contentSub2"></div>

				<div class="mw-parser-output">
					<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Hill of
						loose sand built by aeolian processes or the flow of water</div>
					<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">
						.mw-parser-output .hatnote {
							font-style: italic
						}

						.mw-parser-output div.hatnote {

					<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For the fiction franchise, see <a
							href="/wiki/Dune_(franchise)" title="Dune (franchise)">Dune (franchise)</a>. For other uses,
						see <a href="/wiki/Dune_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Dune (disambiguation)">Dune
							(disambiguation)</a>.</div>

					</p>

					</div>
					<p>A <b>dune</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Landform" title="Landform">landform</a> composed of wind- or
						water-driven <a href="/wiki/Sand" title="Sand">sand</a>. It typically takes the form of a mound,
						ridge, or hill.<sup id="cite_ref-AGI-dune_1-0" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-AGI-dune-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> An area with dunes is called a
						<b>dune system</b><sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> or a <b>dune complex</b>.<sup
							id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJackson1997&quot;Dune_complex&quot;_6-0" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJackson1997&quot;Dune_complex&quot;-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> A
						large dune complex is called a <b>dune field</b>,<sup
							id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJackson1997&quot;Dune_field&quot;_7-0" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJackson1997&quot;Dune_field&quot;-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
						while broad, flat regions covered with wind-swept sand or dunes with little or no vegetation are
						called <i><a href="/wiki/Erg_(landform)" title="Erg (landform)">ergs</a></i> or <i>sand
							seas</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup
							id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJackson1997&quot;Erg&quot;_9-0" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJackson1997&quot;Erg&quot;-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup
							id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJackson1997&quot;Sand_sea&quot;_10-0" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJackson1997&quot;Sand_sea&quot;-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
						Dunes occur in different shapes and sizes, but most kinds of dunes are longer on the <a
							href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/stoss" class="extiw" title="wiktionary:stoss">stoss</a>
						(upflow) side, where the sand is pushed up the dune, and have a shorter <i>slip face</i> in the
						lee side.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJackson1997&quot;Slip_face&quot;_11-0" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJackson1997&quot;Slip_face&quot;-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The
						valley or trough between dunes is called a <i>dune slack</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-12"
							class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
					</p>
					<p>Dunes are most common in desert environments, where the lack of moisture hinders the growth of
						vegetation that would otherwise interfere with the development of dunes. However, sand deposits
						are not restricted to deserts, and dunes are also found along sea shores, along streams in
						semiarid climates, in areas of glacial outwash, and in other areas where poorly cemented <a
							href="/wiki/Sandstone" title="Sandstone">sandstone</a> bedrock disintegrates to produce an
						ample supply of loose sand.<sup id="cite_ref-thornbury-1969_13-0" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-thornbury-1969-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Subaqueous dunes can form
						from the action of water flow (<a href="/wiki/Fluvial" class="mw-redirect"
							title="Fluvial">fluvial</a> processes) on sand or <a href="/wiki/Gravel"
							title="Gravel">gravel</a> beds of <a href="/wiki/River" title="River">rivers</a>, <a
							href="/wiki/Estuary" title="Estuary">estuaries</a>, and the sea-bed.<sup
							id="cite_ref-oxford_14-0" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-oxford-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup
							id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJackson1997&quot;Dune_&#91;streams&#93;&quot;_15-0"
							class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJackson1997&quot;Dune_[streams]&quot;-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
					</p>
					<p>Some coastal areas have one or more sets of dunes running parallel to the shoreline directly
						inland from the <a href="/wiki/Beach" title="Beach">beach</a>. In most cases, the dunes are
						important in protecting the land against potential ravages by storm waves from the <a
							href="/wiki/Sea" title="Sea">sea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Artificial dunes are sometimes constructed
						to protect coastal areas.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The dynamic action of wind and water can
						sometimes cause dunes to drift, which can have serious consequences. For example, the town of <a
							href="/wiki/Eucla,_Western_Australia" title="Eucla, Western Australia">Eucla, Western
							Australia</a>, had to be relocated in the 1890s because of dune drift.<sup id="cite_ref-19"
							class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
					</p>
					<p>
						The modern word "dune" came into English from French around 1790,<sup id="cite_ref-20"
							class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> which in turn came from <a
							href="/wiki/Middle_Dutch" title="Middle Dutch">Middle Dutch</a> <i>dūne</i>.<sup
							id="cite_ref-oxford_14-1" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-oxford-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
						<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">
							.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,
							.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,
							.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,
							.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,
							.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,
							.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul {
								display: none
							}
						</style>
					</p>

										<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-13"><a href="#Dune_complexity"><span
													class="tocnumber">2.2</span> <span class="toctext">Dune
													complexity</span></a></li>
										<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14"><a href="#Dune_movement"><span
													class="tocnumber">2.3</span> <span class="toctext">Dune
													movement</span></a></li>
										<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Coastal_dunes"><span

													dunes</span></a>
											<ul>
												<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-16"><a
														href="#Ecological_succession_on_coastal_dunes"><span

					</div>
					<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation">Formation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span
								class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a
								href="/w/index.php?title=Dune&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1"
								title="Edit section: Formation">edit</a><span
								class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
					<div class="thumb tright">
						<div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Saltation_on_sand_dune.png"
								class="image"><img alt=""
									src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Saltation_on_sand_dune.png/280px-Saltation_on_sand_dune.png"
									decoding="async" width="280" height="107" class="thumbimage"
									srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/56/Saltation_on_sand_dune.png 1.5x"
									data-file-width="337" data-file-height="129" /></a>
							<div class="thumbcaption">
								<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Saltation_on_sand_dune.png" class="internal"
										title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sand hitting sand is more likely to stick; sand
								hitting a more coherent surface is more likely to bounce (<a
									href="/wiki/Saltation_(geology)" title="Saltation (geology)">saltation</a>). This
								exacerbating <a href="/wiki/Feedback_loop" class="mw-redirect"
									title="Feedback loop">feedback loop</a> helps sand accumulate into dunes.
							</div>
						</div>
					</div>
					<p>A universally precise distinction does not exist between ripples, dunes, and a <a
							href="#Draa">draa</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Leeder2012_21-0" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-Leeder2012-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> which are all deposits of the
						same type of materials. Dunes are generally defined as greater than 7&#160;cm tall and may have
						ripples, while ripples are deposits that are less than 3&#160;cm tall.<sup
							id="cite_ref-PettijohnPotter2012_22-0" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-PettijohnPotter2012-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> A draa is a very large
						eolian landform, with a length of several kilometers and a height of tens to hundreds of meters,
						and which may have superimposed dunes.<sup
							id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJackson1997&quot;Draa&quot;_23-0" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJackson1997&quot;Draa&quot;-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
					</p>
					<p><span class="anchor" id="Slip_face"></span>
						Dunes are made of sand-sized particles, and may consist of quartz, calcium carbonate, snow,
						gypsum, or other materials. The upwind/upstream/upcurrent side of the dune is called the stoss
						side; the downflow side is called the lee side. Sand is pushed (creep) or bounces (<a
							href="/wiki/Saltation_(geology)" title="Saltation (geology)">saltation</a>) up the stoss
						side, and slides down the lee side. A side of a dune that the sand has slid down is called a
						<b>slip face</b> (or slipface).
					</p>
					<p>The <a href="/wiki/Bagnold_formula" title="Bagnold formula">Bagnold formula</a> gives the speed
						at which particles can be transported.
					</p>
					<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Aeolian_dunes">Aeolian dunes</span><span
							class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a
								href="/w/index.php?title=Dune&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2"
								title="Edit section: Aeolian dunes">edit</a><span
								class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
					<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aeolian_dune_shapes">Aeolian dune shapes</span><span
							class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a
								href="/w/index.php?title=Dune&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3"
								title="Edit section: Aeolian dune shapes">edit</a><span
								class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
					<p>Five basic dune types are recognized: crescentic, linear, star, dome, and parabolic. Dune areas
						may occur in three forms: simple (isolated dunes of basic type), compound (larger dunes on which
						smaller dunes of same type form), and complex (combinations of different types).<sup
							id="cite_ref-USG_Types_of_Dunes_24-0" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-USG_Types_of_Dunes-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
					</p>
					<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Barchan_or_crescentic">Barchan or crescentic</span><span
							class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a
								href="/w/index.php?title=Dune&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4"
								title="Edit section: Barchan or crescentic">edit</a><span
								class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
					<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" />
					<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Barchan"
							title="Barchan">barchan</a></div>
					<div class="thumb tright">
						<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Barchan_in_Noachis.jpg"
								class="image"><img alt=""
									src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Barchan_in_Noachis.jpg/220px-Barchan_in_Noachis.jpg"
									decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage"
									srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Barchan_in_Noachis.jpg/330px-Barchan_in_Noachis.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Barchan_in_Noachis.jpg/440px-Barchan_in_Noachis.jpg 2x"
									data-file-width="500" data-file-height="333" /></a>
							<div class="thumbcaption">
								<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Barchan_in_Noachis.jpg" class="internal"
										title="Enlarge"></a></div>Isolated barchan dunes on the surface of <a
									href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>. Dominant wind direction would be from left
								to right.
							</div>
						</div>
					</div>
					<p>Barchan dunes are crescent-shaped mounds which are generally wider than they are long. The
						lee-side slipfaces are on the concave sides of the dunes. These dunes form under winds that blow
						consistently from one direction (unimodal
						winds). They form separate crescents when the sand supply is comparatively small. When the sand
						supply is greater, they may merge into barchanoid ridges, and then transverse dunes (see below).
					</p>
					<p>Some types of crescentic dunes move more quickly over <a href="/wiki/Desert"
							title="Desert">desert</a> surfaces than any other type of dune. A group of dunes moved more
						than 100 metres per year between 1954 and 1959 in <a href="/wiki/China"
							title="China">China</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Ningxia_Province" class="mw-redirect"
							title="Ningxia Province">Ningxia Province</a>, and similar speeds have been recorded in the
						<a href="/wiki/Western_Desert_(Egypt)" title="Western Desert (Egypt)">Western Desert</a> of <a
							href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>. The largest crescentic dunes on Earth, with mean
						crest-to-crest widths of more than three kilometres, are in China's <a
							href="/wiki/Taklamakan_Desert" title="Taklamakan Desert">Taklamakan Desert</a>.<sup
							id="cite_ref-USG_Types_of_Dunes_24-1" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-USG_Types_of_Dunes-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
					</p>
					<p>See lunettes and parabolic dunes, below, for dunes similar to crescent-shaped ones.
					</p>
					<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Transverse_dunes">Transverse dunes</span><span
							class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a
								href="/w/index.php?title=Dune&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5"
								title="Edit section: Transverse dunes">edit</a><span
								class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
					<p>Abundant barchan dunes may merge into barchanoid ridges, which then grade into linear (or
						slightly sinuous) transverse dunes, so called because they lie transverse, or across, the wind
						direction, with the wind blowing perpendicular to the ridge crest.<sup
							id="cite_ref-Mangimeli_25-0" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-Mangimeli-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
					</p>
					<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Seif_or_longitudinal_dunes">Seif or longitudinal dunes</span><span
							class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a
								href="/w/index.php?title=Dune&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6"
								title="Edit section: Seif or longitudinal dunes">edit</a><span
								class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
					<p>Seif dunes are linear (or slightly sinuous) dunes with two slip faces.<sup
							id="cite_ref-Mangimeli_25-1" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-Mangimeli-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The two slip faces make them
						sharp-crested. They are called <i>seif</i> dunes after the Arabic word for "sword". They may be
						more than 160 kilometres (100 miles) long, and thus easily visible in satellite images (see
						illustrations).
					</p>
					<p>Seif dunes are associated with bidirectional winds. The long axes and ridges of these dunes
						extend along the resultant direction of sand movement (hence the name "longitudinal").<sup
							id="cite_ref-seif_terminology_26-0" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-seif_terminology-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Some linear dunes merge to
						form Y-shaped compound dunes.<sup id="cite_ref-USG_Types_of_Dunes_24-2" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-USG_Types_of_Dunes-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
					</p>
					<p>Formation is debated. <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Bagnold" title="Ralph Bagnold">Ralph Bagnold</a>, in
						<i><a href="/wiki/The_Physics_of_Blown_Sand_and_Desert_Dunes"
								title="The Physics of Blown Sand and Desert Dunes">The Physics of Blown Sand and Desert
								Dunes</a></i>, suggested that some seif dunes form when a barchan dune moves into a
						bidirectional wind regime, and one arm or wing of the crescent elongates. Others suggest that
						seif dunes are formed by <a href="/wiki/Vortex" title="Vortex">vortices</a> in a unidirectional
						wind.<sup id="cite_ref-Mangimeli_25-2" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-Mangimeli-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> In the sheltered troughs between
						highly developed seif dunes, barchans may be formed, because the wind is constrained to be
						unidirectional by the dunes.
					</p>
					<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
						<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 134.66666666667px">
							<div style="width: 134.66666666667px">
								<div class="thumb" style="width: 132.66666666667px;">
									<div style="margin:0px auto;"><a
											href="/wiki/File:Rub%27_al_Khali_(Arabian_Empty_Quarter)_sand_dunes_imaged_by_Terra_(EOS_AM-1).jpg"
											class="image"
											title="Rub&#39; al Khali (Arabian Empty Quarter) sand dunes imaged by Terra (EOS AM-1). Most of these dunes are seif dunes. Their origin from barchans is suggested by the stubby remnant &quot;hooks&quot; seen on many of the dunes. Wind would be from left to right."><img

						</li>
					</ul>
					<p>Seif dunes are common in the Sahara. They range up to 300&#160;m (980&#160;ft) in height and
						300&#160;km (190&#160;mi) in length. In the southern third of the Arabian Peninsula, a vast <a
							href="/wiki/Erg_(landform)" title="Erg (landform)">erg</a>, called the <a
							href="/wiki/Rub%27_al_Khali" title="Rub&#39; al Khali">Rub' al Khali</a> or Empty Quarter,
						contains seif dunes that stretch for almost 200&#160;km (120&#160;mi) and reach heights of over
						300&#160;m (980&#160;ft).
					</p>
					<p>Linear <a href="/wiki/Loess" title="Loess">loess</a> hills known as <a
							href="/wiki/Paha_(landform)" title="Paha (landform)">pahas</a> are superficially similar.
						These hills appear to have been formed during the last <a href="/wiki/Quaternary_glaciation"
							title="Quaternary glaciation">ice age</a> under <a href="/wiki/Permafrost"
							title="Permafrost">permafrost</a> conditions dominated by sparse <a href="/wiki/Tundra"
							title="Tundra">tundra</a> vegetation.
					</p>
					<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Star">Star</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span
								class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a
								href="/w/index.php?title=Dune&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7"
								title="Edit section: Star">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span>
					</h4>
					<p>Radially symmetrical, star dunes are pyramidal sand mounds with slipfaces on three or more arms
						that radiate from the high center of the mound. They tend to accumulate in areas with
						multidirectional wind regimes. Star dunes grow upward rather than laterally. They dominate the
						<a href="/wiki/Grand_Erg_Oriental" title="Grand Erg Oriental">Grand Erg Oriental</a> of the
						Sahara. In other deserts, they occur around the margins of the <a href="/wiki/Sand_sea"
							class="mw-redirect" title="Sand sea">sand seas</a>, particularly near topographic barriers.
						In the southeast <a href="/wiki/Badain_Jaran_Desert" title="Badain Jaran Desert">Badain Jaran
							Desert</a> of China, the star dunes are up to 500 metres tall and may be the tallest dunes
						on Earth.
					</p>
					<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Dome">Dome</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span
								class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a
								href="/w/index.php?title=Dune&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8"
								title="Edit section: Dome">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span>
					</h4>
					<p>Oval or circular mounds that generally lack a slipface. Dome dunes are rare and occur at the far
						upwind margins of sand seas.
					</p>
					<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Lunettes">Lunettes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span
								class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a
								href="/w/index.php?title=Dune&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9"
								title="Edit section: Lunettes">edit</a><span
								class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
					<p>Fixed crescentic dunes that form on the leeward margins of <a href="/wiki/Dry_lake"
							title="Dry lake">playas</a> and river valleys in arid and semiarid regions in response to
						the direction (s) of prevailing winds, are known as lunettes, source-bordering dunes, bourrelets
						and clay dunes. They may be composed of clay, silt, sand, or gypsum, eroded from the basin floor
						or shore, transported up the concave side of the dune, and deposited on the convex side.
						Examples in Australia are up to 6.5&#160;km long, 1&#160;km wide, and up to 50 metres high. They
						also occur in <a href="/wiki/Southern_Africa" title="Southern Africa">southern</a> and <a
							href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a>, and in parts of the western
						United States, especially Texas.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
					</p>
					<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Parabolic">Parabolic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span
								class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a
								href="/w/index.php?title=Dune&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10"
								title="Edit section: Parabolic">edit</a><span
								class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
					<div class="thumb tright">
						<div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Parabolic_dune.jpg"
								class="image"><img alt=""
									src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Parabolic_dune.jpg/330px-Parabolic_dune.jpg"
									decoding="async" width="330" height="160" class="thumbimage"
									srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Parabolic_dune.jpg/495px-Parabolic_dune.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/09/Parabolic_dune.jpg 2x"
									data-file-width="603" data-file-height="293" /></a>
							<div class="thumbcaption">
								<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Parabolic_dune.jpg" class="internal"
										title="Enlarge"></a></div>Schematic of coastal parabolic dunes
							</div>
						</div>
					</div>
					<p>U-shaped mounds of sand with convex noses trailed by elongated arms are parabolic dunes. These
						dunes are formed from blowout dunes where the erosion of vegetated sand leads to a U-shaped
						depression. The elongated arms are held in place by vegetation; the largest arm known on Earth
						reaches 12&#160;km. Sometimes these dunes are called U-shaped, <a href="/wiki/Blowout_(geology)"
							class="mw-redirect" title="Blowout (geology)">blowout</a>, or hairpin dunes, and they are
						well known in coastal deserts. Unlike crescent shaped dunes, their crests point upwind. The bulk
						of the sand in the dune migrates forward.
					</p>
					<p>In plan view, these are U-shaped or V-shaped mounds of well-sorted, very fine to medium sand with
						elongated arms that extend upwind behind the central part of the dune. There are slipfaces that
						often occur on the outer side of the nose and on the outer slopes of the arms.
					</p>
					<p>These dunes often occur in semiarid areas where the precipitation is retained in the lower parts
						of the dune and underlying <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soils</a>. The stability of the
						dunes was once attributed to the vegetative cover but recent research has pointed to water as
						the main source of parabolic dune stability. The vegetation that covers them—grasses, shrubs,
						and trees—help anchor the trailing arms. In inland deserts, parabolic dunes commonly originate
						and extend downwind from blowouts in sand sheets only partly anchored by vegetation. They can
						also originate from beach sands and extend inland into vegetated areas in coastal zones and on
						shores of large lakes.
					</p>
					<p>Most parabolic dunes do not reach heights higher than a few tens of metres except at their nose,
						where vegetation stops or slows the advance of accumulating sand.
					</p>
					<p>Simple parabolic dunes have only one set of arms that trail upwind, behind the leading nose.
						Compound parabolic dunes are coalesced features with several sets of trailing arms. Complex
						parabolic dunes include subsidiary superposed or coalesced forms, usually of barchanoid or
						linear shapes.
					</p>
					<p>Parabolic dunes, like crescent dunes, occur in areas where very strong winds are mostly
						unidirectional. Although these dunes are found in areas now characterized by variable wind
						speeds, the effective winds associated with the growth and migration of both the parabolic and
						crescent dunes probably are the most consistent in wind direction.
					</p>
					<p>The grain size for these well-sorted, very fine to medium sands is about 0.06 to 0.5&#160;mm.
						Parabolic dunes have loose sand and steep slopes only on their outer flanks. The inner slopes
						are mostly well packed and anchored by vegetation, as are the corridors between individual
						dunes. Because all dune arms are oriented in the same direction, and, the inter-dune corridors
						are generally swept clear of loose sand, the corridors can usually be traversed in between the
						trailing arms of the dune. However to cross straight over the dune by going over the trailing
						arms, can be very difficult. Also, traversing the nose is very difficult as well because the
						nose is usually made up of loose sand without much if any vegetation.
					</p>
					<p>A type of extensive parabolic dune that lacks discernible slipfaces and has mostly coarse grained
						sand is known as a <i>zibar</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> The term zibar comes from the <a
							href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> word to describe "rolling transverse ridges
						... with a hard surface".<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> The dunes are small, have low relief, and
						can be found in many places across the planet from <a href="/wiki/Wyoming"
							title="Wyoming">Wyoming</a> (United States) to Saudi Arabia to Australia.<sup
							id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Spacing
						between zibars ranges from 50 to 400 metres and they don't become more than 10 metres high.<sup
							id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> The dunes
						form at about ninety degrees to the prevailing wind which blows away the small, fine-grained
						sand leaving behind the coarser grained sand to form the crest.<sup id="cite_ref-32"
							class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
					</p>
					<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Reversing_dunes">Reversing dunes</span><span
							class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a
								href="/w/index.php?title=Dune&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11"
								title="Edit section: Reversing dunes">edit</a><span
								class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
					<div class="thumb tleft">
						<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a
								href="/wiki/File:Libya_4608_Idehan_Ubari_Dunes_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg" class="image"><img
									alt=""
									src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Libya_4608_Idehan_Ubari_Dunes_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg/220px-Libya_4608_Idehan_Ubari_Dunes_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg"
									decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage"
									srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Libya_4608_Idehan_Ubari_Dunes_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg/330px-Libya_4608_Idehan_Ubari_Dunes_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Libya_4608_Idehan_Ubari_Dunes_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg/440px-Libya_4608_Idehan_Ubari_Dunes_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg 2x"
									data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="1333" /></a>
							<div class="thumbcaption">
								<div class="magnify"><a
										href="/wiki/File:Libya_4608_Idehan_Ubari_Dunes_Luca_Galuzzi_2007.jpg"
										class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Reversing dune showing short minor
								slipface atop the major stoss (upwind) face
							</div>
						</div>
					</div>
					<p>Occurring wherever winds periodically reverse direction, reversing dunes are varieties of any of
						the above shapes. These dunes typically have major and minor slipfaces oriented in opposite
						directions. The minor slipfaces are usually temporary, as they appear after a reverse wind and
						are generally destroyed when the wind next blows in the dominant direction.<sup
							id="cite_ref-Mangimeli_25-3" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-Mangimeli-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
					</p>
					<p><span class="anchor" id="Draa"></span>
					</p>
					<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Draas">Draas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span
								class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a
								href="/w/index.php?title=Dune&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12"
								title="Edit section: Draas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span>
					</h4>
					<div class="thumb tright">
						<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a
								href="/wiki/File:350m_D%C3%BCne_Deadvlei_Sossusvlei.JPG" class="image"><img alt=""
									src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/350m_D%C3%BCne_Deadvlei_Sossusvlei.JPG/220px-350m_D%C3%BCne_Deadvlei_Sossusvlei.JPG"
									decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage"
									srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/350m_D%C3%BCne_Deadvlei_Sossusvlei.JPG/330px-350m_D%C3%BCne_Deadvlei_Sossusvlei.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/350m_D%C3%BCne_Deadvlei_Sossusvlei.JPG/440px-350m_D%C3%BCne_Deadvlei_Sossusvlei.JPG 2x"
									data-file-width="2272" data-file-height="1704" /></a>
							<div class="thumbcaption">
								<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:350m_D%C3%BCne_Deadvlei_Sossusvlei.JPG"
										class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dune Nine in <a
									href="/wiki/Sossusvlei" title="Sossusvlei">Sossusvlei</a>, Namibia, is over 300m
								high.
							</div>
						</div>
					</div>
					<p>Draas are very large-scale dune bedforms; they may be tens or a few hundreds of metres in height,
						kilometres wide, and hundreds of kilometres in length.<sup id="cite_ref-Mangimeli_25-4"
							class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mangimeli-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> After a draa has
						reached a certain size, it generally develops superimposed dune forms.<sup
							id="cite_ref-Lancaster_33-0" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-Lancaster-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> They are thought to be more
						ancient and slower-moving than smaller dunes,<sup id="cite_ref-Mangimeli_25-5"
							class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mangimeli-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> and to form by
						vertical growth of existing dunes. Draas are widespread in sand seas and are <a
							href="/wiki/Cross-bedding" title="Cross-bedding">well-represented in the geological
							record</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lancaster_33-1" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-Lancaster-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
					</p>
					<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dune_complexity">Dune complexity</span><span
							class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a
								href="/w/index.php?title=Dune&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13"
								title="Edit section: Dune complexity">edit</a><span
								class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
					<p>All these dune shapes may occur in three forms: simple (isolated dunes of basic type), compound
						(larger dunes on which smaller dunes of same type form), and complex (combinations of different
						types).<sup id="cite_ref-USG_Types_of_Dunes_24-3" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-USG_Types_of_Dunes-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Simple dunes are basic
						forms with the minimum number of slipfaces that define the geometric type. Compound dunes are
						large dunes on which smaller dunes of similar type and slipface orientation are superimposed.
						Complex dunes are combinations of two or more dune types. A crescentic dune with a star dune
						superimposed on its crest is the most common complex dune. Simple dunes represent a wind regime
						that has not changed in intensity or direction since the formation of the dune, while compound
						and complex dunes suggest that the intensity and direction of the wind has changed.
					</p>
					<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dune_movement">Dune movement</span><span
							class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a
								href="/w/index.php?title=Dune&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14"
								title="Edit section: Dune movement">edit</a><span
								class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
					<p>The sand mass of dunes can move either windward or leeward, depending on if the wind is making
						contact with the dune from below or above its apogee. If wind hits from above, the sand
						particles move leeward; the leeward flux of sand is greater than the windward flux. Conversely,
						if sand hits from below, sand particles move windward. Further, if the wind is carrying sand
						particles when it hits the dune, the dune's sand particles will saltate more than if the wind
						had hit the dune without carrying sand particles.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
					</p>
					<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coastal_dunes">Coastal dunes</span><span
							class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a
								href="/w/index.php?title=Dune&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15"
								title="Edit section: Coastal dunes">edit</a><span
								class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
					<div class="thumb tright">
						<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a
								href="/wiki/File:LIVER_AAS_UDLOEB_I_JULI_2012_(ubt)-002.JPG" class="image"><img alt=""
									src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/LIVER_AAS_UDLOEB_I_JULI_2012_%28ubt%29-002.JPG/220px-LIVER_AAS_UDLOEB_I_JULI_2012_%28ubt%29-002.JPG"
									decoding="async" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage"
									srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/LIVER_AAS_UDLOEB_I_JULI_2012_%28ubt%29-002.JPG/330px-LIVER_AAS_UDLOEB_I_JULI_2012_%28ubt%29-002.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/LIVER_AAS_UDLOEB_I_JULI_2012_%28ubt%29-002.JPG/440px-LIVER_AAS_UDLOEB_I_JULI_2012_%28ubt%29-002.JPG 2x"
									data-file-width="4272" data-file-height="2411" /></a>
							<div class="thumbcaption">
								<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:LIVER_AAS_UDLOEB_I_JULI_2012_(ubt)-002.JPG"
										class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coastal dunes covered in grasses
								around the mouth of the Liver Å river in Denmark
							</div>
						</div>
					</div>

							</video>
							<div class="thumbcaption">
								<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Newborough_Dune_Rejuvenation.webm"
										class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Newborough Dune Rejuvenation, Wales;
								video of work done by <a href="/wiki/Natural_Resources_Wales"
									title="Natural Resources Wales">Natural Resources Wales</a>; 2015
							</div>
						</div>
					</div>
					<p>Coastal dunes<sup id="cite_ref-sloss-etal-2012_35-0" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-sloss-etal-2012-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> form when wet sand is
						deposited along the coast and dries out and is blown along the beach.<sup id="cite_ref-36"
							class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Dunes form where the beach
						is wide enough to allow for the accumulation of wind-blown sand, and where prevailing <b>onshore
							winds</b> tend to blow sand inland. The three key ingredients for coastal dune formation are
						a large sand supply, winds to move said sand supply, and a place for the sand supply to
						accumulate.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Obstacles—for example, vegetation, pebbles
						and so on—tend to slow down the wind and lead to the deposition of sand grains.<sup
							id="cite_ref-Hesp1989_38-0" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-Hesp1989-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> These small "incipient dunes or
						"shadow dunes" tend to grow in the vertical direction if the obstacle slowing the wind can also
						grow vertically (i.e., vegetation). Coastal dunes expand laterally as a result of lateral growth
						of coastal plants via seed or <a href="/wiki/Rhizome" title="Rhizome">rhizome</a>.<sup
							id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup
							id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Models of
						coastal dunes suggest that their final equilibrium height is related to the distance between the
						water line and where vegetation can grow.<sup id="cite_ref-DuranMoore2013_41-0"
							class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DuranMoore2013-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Coastal
						dunes can be classified by where they develop, or begin to take shape. Dunes are commonly
						grouped into either the Primary Dune Group or the Secondary Dune Group.<sup
							id="cite_ref-sloss-etal-2012_35-1" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-sloss-etal-2012-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Primary dunes gain most of
						their sand from the beach itself, while secondary dunes gain their sand from the primary dune.
						Along the Florida Panhandle, most dunes are considered to be foredunes or hummocks.<sup
							id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup
							id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Different
						locations around the globe have dune formations unique to their given coastal profile.
					</p>
					<p>Coastal sand dunes can provide privacy and/or habitats to support local flora and fauna. Animals
						such as sand snakes, lizards, and rodents can live in coastal sand dunes, along with insects of
						all types.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
						Often the vegetation of sand dunes is discussed without acknowledging the importance that
						coastal dunes have for animals. Further, some animals, such as foxes and feral pigs can use
						coastal dunes as hunting grounds to find food.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Birds are also known to utilize coastal
						dunes as nesting grounds. All these species find the coastal environment of the sand dune vital
						to their species' survival.
					</p>
					<p>Over the course of time coastal dunes may be impacted by <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone"
							title="Tropical cyclone">tropical cyclones</a> or other intense storm activity, dependent on
						their location. Recent work has suggested that coastal dunes tend to evolve toward a high or low
						morphology depending on the growth rate of dunes relative to storm frequency.<sup
							id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup
							id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> During a
						storm event, dunes play a significant role in minimizing wave energy as it moves onshore. As a
						result, coastal dunes, especially those in the foredune area affected by a <a
							href="/wiki/Storm_surge" title="Storm surge">storm surge</a>, will retreat or erode.<sup
							id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> To
						counteract the damage from tropical activity on coastal dunes, short term post-storm efforts can
						be made by individual agencies through fencing to help with sand accumulation.<sup
							id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
					</p>
					<p>How much a dune erodes during any storm surge is related to its location on the coastal shoreline
						and the profile of the beach during a particular season. In those areas with harsher winter
						weather, during the summer a beach tends to take on more of a convex appearance due to gentler
						waves, while the same beach in the winter may take on more of a concave appearance. As a result,
						coastal dunes can get eroded much more quickly in the winter than in the summer. The converse is
						true in areas with harsher summer weather.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
					</p>
					<p>There are many threats to these coastal communities. Some coastal dunes, for example ones in San
						Francisco, have been completely altered by urbanization; reshaping the dune for human use. This
						puts native species at risk. Another danger, in California and places in the UK specifically, is
						the introduction of invasive species. Plant species, such as <i><a
								href="/wiki/Carpobrotus_edulis" title="Carpobrotus edulis">Carpobrotus edulis</a></i>,
						were introduced from South Africa in an attempt to stabilize the dunes and provide horticultural
						benefits, but instead spread taking land away from native species. <i><a
								href="/wiki/Ammophila_arenaria" title="Ammophila arenaria">Ammophila arenaria</a></i>,
						known as European beachgrass, has a similar story, though it has no horticulture benefits. It
						has great ground coverage and, as intended, stabilized the dunes but as an unintended side
						effect prevented native species from thriving in those dunes. One such example is the dune field
						at <a href="/wiki/Point_Reyes" title="Point Reyes">Point Reyes, California</a>. There are now
						efforts to get rid of both of these invasive species.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
					</p>
					<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecological_succession_on_coastal_dunes">Ecological succession on
							coastal dunes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span
								class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a
								href="/w/index.php?title=Dune&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16"
								title="Edit section: Ecological succession on coastal dunes">edit</a><span
								class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
					<p>As a dune forms, plant <a href="/wiki/Ecological_succession"
							title="Ecological succession">succession</a> occurs. The conditions on an <i>embryo dune</i>
						are harsh, with <a href="/wiki/Salt_spray" class="mw-redirect" title="Salt spray">salt spray</a>
						from the sea carried on strong winds. The dune is well drained and often dry, and composed of
						calcium carbonate from seashells. Rotting <a href="/wiki/Seaweed" title="Seaweed">seaweed</a>,
						brought in by storm waves adds nutrients to allow <a href="/wiki/Pioneer_species"
							title="Pioneer species">pioneer species</a> to colonize the dune. For example, in the <a
							href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> these pioneer species
						are often <a href="/wiki/Ammophila_(Poaceae)" class="mw-redirect"
							title="Ammophila (Poaceae)">marram grass</a>, <a
							href="/w/index.php?title=Sea_wort_grass&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new"
							title="Sea wort grass (page does not exist)">sea wort grass</a> and other sea grasses. These
						plants are well adapted to the harsh conditions of the foredune, typically having deep roots
						which reach the <a href="/wiki/Water_table" title="Water table">water table</a>, <a
							href="/wiki/Root_nodules" class="mw-redirect" title="Root nodules">root nodules</a> that
						produce <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> compounds, and protected <a
							href="/wiki/Stoma" title="Stoma">stoma</a>, reducing <a href="/wiki/Transpiration"
							title="Transpiration">transpiration</a>. Also, the deep roots bind the sand together, and
						the dune grows into a <a href="/wiki/Foredune" title="Foredune">foredune</a> as more sand is
						blown over the grasses. The grasses add <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>
						to the soil, meaning other, less hardy plants can then colonize the dunes. Typically these are
						<a href="/wiki/Calluna" title="Calluna">heather</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heath_(habitat)"
							class="mw-redirect" title="Heath (habitat)">heaths</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gorse"
							class="mw-redirect" title="Gorse">gorses</a>. These too are adapted to the low <a
							href="/wiki/Soil#Soil_moisture" title="Soil">soil water</a> content and have small, prickly
						leaves which reduce transpiration. Heather adds <a href="/wiki/Humus" title="Humus">humus</a> to
						the soil and is usually replaced by <a href="/wiki/Conifer" title="Conifer">coniferous</a>
						trees, which can tolerate low <a href="/wiki/Soil_pH" title="Soil pH">soil pH</a>, caused by the
						accumulation and decomposition of organic matter with nitrate leaching.<sup
							id="cite_ref-Miles1985_53-0" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-Miles1985-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Coniferous forests and <a
							href="/wiki/Heathland" class="mw-redirect" title="Heathland">heathland</a> are common <a
							href="/wiki/Climax_community" title="Climax community">climax communities</a> for sand dune
						systems.
					</p>
					<p>Young dunes are called <a href="/wiki/Yellow_dune" title="Yellow dune">yellow dunes</a> and dunes
						which have high humus content are called <a href="/wiki/Grey_dune" title="Grey dune">grey
							dunes</a>. Leaching occurs on the dunes, washing humus into the slacks, and the slacks may
						be much more developed than the exposed tops of the dunes. It is usually in the slacks that more
						rare species are developed and there is a tendency for the dune slacks' soil to be waterlogged
						where only marsh plants can survive. In Europe these plants include: creeping willow, cotton
						grass, <a href="/wiki/Yellow_iris" class="mw-redirect" title="Yellow iris">yellow iris</a>,
						reeds, and rushes. As for vertebrates in European dunes, <a href="/wiki/Natterjack_toad"
							title="Natterjack toad">natterjack toads</a> sometimes breed here.
					</p>
					<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Coastal_dune_floral_adaptations">Coastal dune floral
							adaptations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span
								class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a
								href="/w/index.php?title=Dune&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17"
								title="Edit section: Coastal dune floral adaptations">edit</a><span
								class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
					<div class="thumb tright">
						<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a

					</div>

							<div class="thumbcaption">
								<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sea_dune_Erosion_at_Talace,_Wales.webm"
										class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sea dune erosion at <a
									href="/wiki/Talacre" title="Talacre">Talacre</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wales"
									title="Wales">Wales</a>
							</div>
						</div>
					</div>
					<p>Dune ecosystems are extremely difficult places for plants to survive. This is due to a number of
						pressures related to their proximity to the ocean and confinement to growth on sandy substrates.
						These include:
					</p>
					<ul>
						<li>Little available soil moisture</li>
						<li>Little available <a href="/wiki/Soil_organic_matter" title="Soil organic matter">soil
								organic matter</a>/nutrients/water</li>
						<li>Harsh winds</li>
						<li>Salt spray</li>
						<li>Erosion/shifting and sometimes burial or exposure (from shifting)</li>
						<li>Tidal influences</li>
					</ul>
					<p>There are many adaptations plants have evolved to cope with these pressures:
					</p>
					<ul>
						<li>Deep taproot to reach water table (<a href="/wiki/Abronia_umbellata"
								title="Abronia umbellata">Pink Sand Verbena</a>)</li>
						<li>Shallow but extensive root systems</li>
						<li><a href="/wiki/Rhizome" title="Rhizome">Rhizomes</a></li>
						<li>Prostrate growth form to avoid wind/salt spray (<i>Abronia spp</i>., Beach Primrose)</li>
						<li><a href="/wiki/Krummholz" title="Krummholz">Krummholz</a> growth form (<a
								href="/wiki/Cupressus_macrocarpa" title="Cupressus macrocarpa">Monterrey Cypress</a>-not
							a dune plant but deals with similar pressures)</li>
						<li>Thickened cuticle/Succulence to reduce moisture loss and reduce salt uptake
							(<i>Ambrosia/Abronia spp., Calystegia soldanella</i>)</li>
						<li>Pale leaves to reduce insolation (<i>Artemisia/Ambrosia spp.</i>)</li>
						<li>Thorny/Spiky seeds to ensure establishment in vicinity of parent, reduces chances of being
							blown away or swept out to sea (<i>Ambrosia chamissonis</i>)</li>
					</ul>
					<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gypsum_dunes">Gypsum dunes</span><span
							class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a
								href="/w/index.php?title=Dune&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18"
								title="Edit section: Gypsum dunes">edit</a><span
								class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
					<div class="thumb tright">
						<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a
								href="/wiki/File:Aerial_view_of_dunefield,_White_Sands_National_Park,_New_Mexico,_United_States.png"
								class="image"><img alt=""
									src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Aerial_view_of_dunefield%2C_White_Sands_National_Park%2C_New_Mexico%2C_United_States.png/220px-Aerial_view_of_dunefield%2C_White_Sands_National_Park%2C_New_Mexico%2C_United_States.png"
									decoding="async" width="220" height="97" class="thumbimage"
									srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Aerial_view_of_dunefield%2C_White_Sands_National_Park%2C_New_Mexico%2C_United_States.png/330px-Aerial_view_of_dunefield%2C_White_Sands_National_Park%2C_New_Mexico%2C_United_States.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Aerial_view_of_dunefield%2C_White_Sands_National_Park%2C_New_Mexico%2C_United_States.png/440px-Aerial_view_of_dunefield%2C_White_Sands_National_Park%2C_New_Mexico%2C_United_States.png 2x"
									data-file-width="1051" data-file-height="462" /></a>
							<div class="thumbcaption">
								<div class="magnify"><a
										href="/wiki/File:Aerial_view_of_dunefield,_White_Sands_National_Park,_New_Mexico,_United_States.png"
										class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gypsum dune fields, <a
									href="/wiki/White_Sands_National_Park" title="White Sands National Park">White Sands
									National Park</a>, New Mexico, United States
							</div>
						</div>
					</div>
					<p>In deserts where large amounts of limestone mountains surround a <a href="/wiki/Closed_basin"
							class="mw-redirect" title="Closed basin">closed basin</a>, such as at <a
							href="/wiki/White_Sands_National_Park" title="White Sands National Park">White Sands
							National Park</a> in south-central <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New
							Mexico</a>, occasional storm runoff transports dissolved limestone and <a
							href="/wiki/Gypsum" title="Gypsum">gypsum</a> into a low-lying pan within the basin where
						the water evaporates, depositing the gypsum and forming crystals known as <a
							href="/wiki/Selenite_(mineral)" title="Selenite (mineral)">selenite</a>. The crystals left
						behind by this process are eroded by the wind and deposited as vast white dune fields that
						resemble snow-covered landscapes. These types of dune are rare, and only form in closed arid
						basins that retain the highly soluble gypsum that would otherwise be washed into the sea.<sup
							id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
					</p>
					<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nabkha_dunes">Nabkha dunes</span><span
							class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a
								href="/w/index.php?title=Dune&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19"
								title="Edit section: Nabkha dunes">edit</a><span
								class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
					<p>A <a href="/wiki/Nabkha" title="Nabkha">nabkha</a>, or coppice dune, is a small dune anchored by
						vegetation. They usually indicate desertification or soil erosion, and serve as nesting and
						burrow sites for animals.
					</p>
					<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sub-aqueous_dunes">Sub-aqueous dunes</span><span
							class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a
								href="/w/index.php?title=Dune&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20"
								title="Edit section: Sub-aqueous dunes">edit</a><span
								class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
					<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" />
					<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a
							href="/wiki/Ripple_marks" title="Ripple marks">Ripple marks</a></div>
					<p>Sub-aqueous (<a href="/wiki/Underwater" class="mw-redirect" title="Underwater">underwater</a>)
						dunes form on a bed of sand or gravel under the actions of water flow. They are ubiquitous in
						natural <a href="/wiki/Channel_(geography)" title="Channel (geography)">channels</a> such as
						rivers and estuaries, and also form in engineered <a href="/wiki/Canal" title="Canal">canals</a>
						and pipelines.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Dunes move downstream as the upstream slope
						is eroded and the sediment deposited on the downstream or lee slope in typical <a
							href="/wiki/Bedform" title="Bedform">bedform</a> construction.<sup id="cite_ref-58"
							class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> In the case of sub-aqueous
						barchan dunes, sediment is lost by their extremities, known as horns.<sup id="cite_ref-59"
							class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60"
							class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
					</p>
					<p>These dunes most often form as a continuous 'train' of dunes, showing remarkable similarity in <a
							href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">wavelength</a> and height. The shape of a dune
						gives information about its formation environment.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> For instance, rivers produce asymmetrical
						ripples, with the steeper slip face facing downstream. Ripple marks preserved in sedimentary
						strata in the geological record can be used to determine the direction of current flow, and thus
						an indication of the source of the sediments.
					</p>
					<p>Dunes on the bed of a channel significantly increase flow resistance, their presence and growth
						playing a major part in river <a href="/wiki/Flooding" class="mw-redirect"
							title="Flooding">flooding</a>.
					</p>
					<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Lithified_dunes">Lithified dunes</span><span
							class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a
								href="/w/index.php?title=Dune&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21"
								title="Edit section: Lithified dunes">edit</a><span
								class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
					<div class="thumb tright">
						<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a
								href="/wiki/File:Cross-bedding_Of_Sandstone_Near_Mt_Carmel_Road_Zion_Canyon_Utah.jpg"
								class="image"><img alt=""
									src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Cross-bedding_Of_Sandstone_Near_Mt_Carmel_Road_Zion_Canyon_Utah.jpg/220px-Cross-bedding_Of_Sandstone_Near_Mt_Carmel_Road_Zion_Canyon_Utah.jpg"
									decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage"
									srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Cross-bedding_Of_Sandstone_Near_Mt_Carmel_Road_Zion_Canyon_Utah.jpg/330px-Cross-bedding_Of_Sandstone_Near_Mt_Carmel_Road_Zion_Canyon_Utah.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Cross-bedding_Of_Sandstone_Near_Mt_Carmel_Road_Zion_Canyon_Utah.jpg/440px-Cross-bedding_Of_Sandstone_Near_Mt_Carmel_Road_Zion_Canyon_Utah.jpg 2x"
									data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a>
							<div class="thumbcaption">
								<div class="magnify"><a
										href="/wiki/File:Cross-bedding_Of_Sandstone_Near_Mt_Carmel_Road_Zion_Canyon_Utah.jpg"
										class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cross-bedding in lithified aeolian
								sand dunes preserved as sandstone in Zion National Park, Utah
							</div>
						</div>
					</div>
					<p>A <a href="/wiki/Lithification" title="Lithification">lithified</a> (consolidated) sand dune is a
						type of <a href="/wiki/Sandstone" title="Sandstone">sandstone</a> that is formed when a marine
						or aeolian sand dune becomes compacted and hardened. Once in this form, water passing through
						the rock can carry and deposit minerals, which can alter the color of the rock. <a
							href="/wiki/Cross-bedding" title="Cross-bedding">Cross-bedded</a> layers of stacks of
						lithified dunes can produce the cross-hatching patterns, such as those seen in <a
							href="/wiki/Zion_National_Park" title="Zion National Park">Zion National Park</a> in the
						western United States.
					</p>
					<p>A slang term, used in the <a href="/wiki/Southwestern_United_States"
							title="Southwestern United States">southwest US</a>, for consolidated and hardened sand
						dunes is "slickrock", a name that was introduced by pioneers of the <a href="/wiki/Old_West"
							class="mw-redirect" title="Old West">Old West</a> because their steel-rimmed wagon wheels
						could not gain traction on the rock.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact"
							style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed"
									title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span
										title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2021)">citation
										needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
					</p>
					<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Desertification">Desertification</span><span
							class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a
								href="/w/index.php?title=Dune&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22"
								title="Edit section: Desertification">edit</a><span
								class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
					<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" />
					<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a
							href="/wiki/Desertification" title="Desertification">Desertification</a></div>
					<p>Sand dunes can have a negative impact on humans when they encroach on human habitats. Sand dunes
						move via a few different means, all of them helped along by wind. One way that dunes can move is
						by <a href="/wiki/Saltation_(geology)" title="Saltation (geology)">saltation</a>, where sand
						particles skip along the ground like a <a href="/wiki/Bouncing_ball"
							title="Bouncing ball">bouncing ball</a>. When these skipping particles land, they may knock
						into other particles and cause them to move as well, in a process known as <a
							href="/w/index.php?title=Creep_(sand_dunes)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new"
							title="Creep (sand dunes) (page does not exist)">creep</a>. With slightly stronger winds,
						particles collide in mid-air, causing <a
							href="/w/index.php?title=Sheet_flow&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new"
							title="Sheet flow (page does not exist)">sheet flows</a>. In a major <a
							href="/wiki/Dust_storm" title="Dust storm">dust storm</a>, dunes may move tens of metres
						through such sheet flows. Also as in the case of snow, sand <a href="/wiki/Avalanche"
							title="Avalanche">avalanches</a>, falling down the <a href="/wiki/Slipface"
							class="mw-redirect" title="Slipface">slipface</a> of the dunes—that face away from the
						winds—also move the dunes forward.
					</p>
					<p>Sand threatens buildings and crops in Africa, the Middle East, and China. Drenching sand dunes
						with oil stops their migration, but this approach uses a valuable resource and is quite
						destructive to the dunes' animal habitats. <a href="/wiki/Sand_fence" title="Sand fence">Sand
							fences</a> might also slow their movement to a crawl, but geologists are still analyzing
						results for the optimum fence designs.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Preventing sand dunes from overwhelming
						towns, villages, and agricultural areas has become a priority for the <a
							href="/wiki/United_Nations_Environment_Programme"
							title="United Nations Environment Programme">United Nations Environment Programme</a>.
						Planting dunes with vegetation also helps to stabilise them.
					</p>
					<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Conservation">Conservation</span><span
							class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a
								href="/w/index.php?title=Dune&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23"
								title="Edit section: Conservation">edit</a><span
								class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
					<div class="thumb tright">
						<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:KelsoSand.JPG"
								class="image"><img alt=""
									src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/KelsoSand.JPG/220px-KelsoSand.JPG"
									decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage"
									srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/KelsoSand.JPG/330px-KelsoSand.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/KelsoSand.JPG/440px-KelsoSand.JPG 2x"
									data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944" /></a>
							<div class="thumbcaption">
								<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:KelsoSand.JPG" class="internal"
										title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sand blowing off a crest in the <a
									href="/wiki/Kelso_Dunes" title="Kelso Dunes">Kelso Dunes</a> of the <a
									href="/wiki/Mojave_Desert" title="Mojave Desert">Mojave Desert</a>, California, USA
							</div>
						</div>
					</div>
					<p>Dune habitats provide niches for highly specialized plants and animals, including numerous <a
							href="/wiki/Rare_species" title="Rare species">rare species</a> and some <a
							href="/wiki/Endangered_species" title="Endangered species">endangered species</a>. Due to
						widespread human population expansion, dunes face destruction through land development and
						recreational usages, as well as alteration to prevent the encroachment of sand onto inhabited
						areas. Some countries, notably the United States, Australia, Canada, New Zealand, the United
						Kingdom, Netherlands, and Sri Lanka have developed significant programs of dune protection
						through the use of <a href="/wiki/Sand_dune_stabilization" title="Sand dune stabilization">sand
							dune stabilization</a>. In the U.K., a <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity_Action_Plan"
							class="mw-redirect" title="Biodiversity Action Plan">Biodiversity Action Plan</a> has been
						developed to assess dunes loss and to prevent future dunes destruction.
					</p>
					<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Examples">Examples</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span
								class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a
								href="/w/index.php?title=Dune&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24"
								title="Edit section: Examples">edit</a><span
								class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

					<ul>
						<li>Victoria Island Sand Dunes,160&#160;km North West of Cambridge Bay, Nunavut, Canada.
							Approximately 600 square kilometers, the largest in Canada, third largest in North America
							and the largest in the Arctic. There are two lakes with direct access to the Dunes from
							float planes.</li>
						<li>Herring Cove,<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a
									href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Race Point<sup id="cite_ref-65"
								class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> and The Province Lands
							bicycle path<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a
									href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> in <a
								href="/wiki/Provincetown,_Massachusetts"
								title="Provincetown, Massachusetts">Provincetown, Massachusetts</a> as part of the <a
								href="/wiki/National_Park_Service" title="National Park Service">US National Park
								Service</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Cape_Cod_National_Seashore"
								title="Cape Cod National Seashore">Cape Cod National Seashore</a>.</li>
						<li>Great Kobuk Sand Dunes, <a href="/wiki/Kobuk_Valley_National_Park"
								title="Kobuk Valley National Park">Kobuk Valley National Park</a>, <a
								href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a><sup id="cite_ref-qsr_67-0"
								class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-qsr-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup></li>
						<li>The Athabasca Sand Dunes, located in the <a
								href="/wiki/Athabasca_Sand_Dunes_Provincial_Park"
								title="Athabasca Sand Dunes Provincial Park">Athabasca Sand Dunes Provincial Park</a>,
							<a href="/wiki/Saskatchewan" title="Saskatchewan">Saskatchewan</a>.</li>
						<li>The <a rel="nofollow" class="external text"
								href="https://web.archive.org/web/20160817155504/http://www.blm.gov/ca/st/en/fo/needles/wilderness/cadiz_dunes.html">Cadiz
								Dunes</a> in the <a rel="nofollow" class="external text"
								href="https://web.archive.org/web/20160216044509/http://www.blm.gov/ca/st/en/prog/nlcs/Mojave_Trails.html">Mojave
								Trails National Monument</a> in <a href="/wiki/California"
								title="California">California</a>.</li>
						<li>The <a href="/wiki/Kelso_Dunes" title="Kelso Dunes">Kelso Dunes</a> in the <a

								href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>.</li>

							in the state of <a href="/wiki/Chihuahua_(state)" title="Chihuahua (state)">Chihuahua</a>,
							<a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a></li>
						<li><a href="/wiki/Algodones_Dunes" title="Algodones Dunes">Algodones Dunes</a> near <a
								href="/wiki/Brawley,_California" title="Brawley, California">Brawley, California</a>.
						</li>
						<li><a href="/wiki/Guadalupe-Nipomo_Dunes" title="Guadalupe-Nipomo Dunes">Guadalupe-Nipomo
								Dunes</a>, on the central coast of <a href="/wiki/California"
								title="California">California</a>.</li>
						<li><a href="/wiki/Monahans_Sandhills_State_Park" title="Monahans Sandhills State Park">Monahans
								Sandhills State Park</a> near <a href="/wiki/Odessa,_Texas"
								title="Odessa, Texas">Odessa, Texas</a>.</li>
						<li><a href="/wiki/Beaver_Dunes_State_Park" class="mw-redirect"
								title="Beaver Dunes State Park">Beaver Dunes State Park</a> near <a
								href="/wiki/Beaver,_Oklahoma" title="Beaver, Oklahoma">Beaver, Oklahoma</a>.</li>
						<li>The Killpecker sand dunes of the <a href="/wiki/Red_Desert_(Wyoming)"
								title="Red Desert (Wyoming)">Red Desert</a> in southwestern <a href="/wiki/Wyoming"
								title="Wyoming">Wyoming</a>.</li>
						<li><a href="/wiki/Jockey%27s_Ridge_State_Park" title="Jockey&#39;s Ridge State Park">Jockey's
								Ridge State Park</a> – on the <a href="/wiki/Outer_Banks" title="Outer Banks">Outer
								Banks</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina" title="North Carolina">North Carolina</a>.
						</li>
						<li>The Great Dune found in <a href="/wiki/Cape_Henlopen_State_Park"
								title="Cape Henlopen State Park">Cape Henlopen State Park</a> in <a
								href="/wiki/Lewes,_Delaware" title="Lewes, Delaware">Lewes, Delaware</a>.</li>
						<li><a href="/wiki/Oregon_Dunes_National_Recreation_Area"
								title="Oregon Dunes National Recreation Area">Oregon Dunes National Recreation Area</a>
							near <a href="/wiki/Florence,_Oregon" title="Florence, Oregon">Florence, Oregon</a>, on the
							Pacific Coast.</li>
						<li><a href="/wiki/Bruneau_Dunes_State_Park" title="Bruneau Dunes State Park">Bruneau Dunes
								State Park</a> – <a href="/wiki/Owyhee_Desert" title="Owyhee Desert">Owyhee Desert</a>,
							<a href="/wiki/Idaho" title="Idaho">Idaho</a></li>
						<li><a href="/wiki/Hoffmaster_State_Park" title="Hoffmaster State Park">Hoffmaster State
								Park</a> – <a href="/wiki/Muskegon" class="mw-redirect" title="Muskegon">Muskegon</a>,
							<a href="/wiki/Michigan" title="Michigan">Michigan</a></li>
						<li><a href="/wiki/Silver_Lake_State_Park_(Michigan)"
								title="Silver Lake State Park (Michigan)">Silver Lake State Park</a> — a sand dunes that
							allows off-road vehicle use located near <a href="/wiki/Mears,_Michigan"
								title="Mears, Michigan">Mears, Michigan</a>.</li>
					</ul>

								href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a></li>
					</ul>
					<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Extraterrestrial_dunes">Extraterrestrial dunes</span><span
							class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a
								href="/w/index.php?title=Dune&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33"
								title="Edit section: Extraterrestrial dunes">edit</a><span
								class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
					<div class="thumb tright">
						<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gusev_Crater,_Mars.jpg"
								class="image"><img alt=""
									src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Gusev_Crater%2C_Mars.jpg/220px-Gusev_Crater%2C_Mars.jpg"
									decoding="async" width="220" height="77" class="thumbimage"
									srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Gusev_Crater%2C_Mars.jpg/330px-Gusev_Crater%2C_Mars.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Gusev_Crater%2C_Mars.jpg/440px-Gusev_Crater%2C_Mars.jpg 2x"
									data-file-width="1456" data-file-height="510" /></a>
							<div class="thumbcaption">
								<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gusev_Crater,_Mars.jpg" class="internal"
										title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sand dune on Mars
							</div>
						</div>
					</div>
					<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" />
					<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a
							href="/wiki/List_of_extraterrestrial_dune_fields"
							title="List of extraterrestrial dune fields">List of extraterrestrial dune fields</a></div>
					<p>Dunes can likely be found in any environment where there is a substantial atmosphere, winds, and
						dust to be blown. Dunes are common on <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> and in the
						equatorial regions of <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a>.
					</p>
					<p>Titan's dunes include large expanses with modal lengths of about 20–30&#160;km. The regions are
						not topographically confined, resembling sand seas. These dunes are interpreted to be
						longitudinal dunes whose crests are oriented parallel to the dominant wind direction, which
						generally indicates west-to-east wind flow. The sand is likely composed of hydrocarbon
						particles, possibly with some water ice mixed in.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
					</p>
					<p>Dunes are a popular theme in science fiction, featuring in depictions of dry <a
							href="/wiki/Desert_planet" title="Desert planet">Desert planets</a><sup
							id="cite_ref-Touponce_7_74-0" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-Touponce_7-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> appearing as early as the 1956
						film <i><a href="/wiki/Forbidden_Planet" title="Forbidden Planet">Forbidden Planet</a></i> and
						<a href="/wiki/Frank_Herbert" title="Frank Herbert">Frank Herbert</a>'s 1965 novel <i><a
								href="/wiki/Dune_(novel)" title="Dune (novel)">Dune</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-75"
							class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup
							id="cite_ref-Hladik_76-0" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-Hladik-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dune_Endures_77-0"
							class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dune_Endures-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> The
						environment of the desert planet <a href="/wiki/Arrakis" title="Arrakis">Arrakis</a> (also known
						as Dune) in the <a href="/wiki/Dune_(franchise)" title="Dune (franchise)"><i>Dune</i>
							franchise</a><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a
								href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> <i>Dune</i> in turn inspired the <i><a
								href="/wiki/Star_Wars" title="Star Wars">Star Wars</a></i> franchise,<sup
							id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> which
						includes prominent theme of dunes on fictional planets such as <a href="/wiki/Tatooine"
							title="Tatooine">Tatooine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geonosis" class="mw-redirect"
							title="Geonosis">Geonosis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jakku" title="Jakku">Jakku</a>.
					</p>
					<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span
								class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a
								href="/w/index.php?title=Dune&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34"
								title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span
								class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
					<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r998391716">
						.mw-parser-output .div-col {
